Division of Cardiology
University of Western Ontario
London, Ontario, Canada</p>
<p>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="foreword">Foreword<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foreword" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The electrophysiology (EP) laboratory at the London Health Sciences Centre in London, Canada was established in 1980. Invasive EP was relatively new at that time and grew from groundbreaking innovations by key pioneers in several early centers of excellence. The ability to record electrograms from specific locations within the heart, including the His bundle, and to use programmed electrical stimulation of the heart to measure conduction parameters and induce arrhythmias was revolutionary. These advances amplified our ability to understand and treat cardiac rhythm disorders over and above what is possible with the electrocardiogram. The ensuing decades saw the introduction of more key innovations, including surgery for rhythm disorders, implantable devices, and most recently, catheter ablation.</p>
<p>Although the fundamental EP study is similar today to that performed in 1980, the broad range of enabling technologies available today has made it into a much more complex exercise requiring an equally broad range of skill sets and well-coordinated teamwork. "Teamwork" is not just a word when it comes to EP studies, where everyone's attention needs to be focused squarely on the job at hand. Because a single observation from any member of the teamâ€”nurse, technologist, physician assistant, or traineeâ€”can make a critical difference in outcome, the entire team needs to be encouraged to stay involved and</p>
<p>understand what is happening rather than just working in their little corner. No one should be afraid to present an observation to the team; in fact, we are all obligated to do so.</p>
<p>New trainees and personnel face a daunting challenge in acclimating to this setting! This may be especially true for technical and nursing staff who have little available to introduce them to the subject matter and the interpretation of the electrogram data in particular. Paul Purves is a unique EP technologist who has coupled his technical expertise and knowledge of EP with a passion for understanding the underpinnings of the study and teaches what he knows to other technologists, nurses, and indeed physicians. He is a gifted teacher who has coordinated and assembled the "collective wisdom" of our team into this reader-friendly and unique visual guide to performing and understanding the arrhythmia study. It surpasses the needs of a simple introduction and will be useful to all levels of trainee who want to understand what is really "going on" and move to the next level.</p>
<p>â€”Dr. George J. Klein</p>
<p>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="preface">Preface<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preface" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Cardiac Electrophysiology: A Visual Guide for Nurses, Techs, and Fellows is just thatâ€”a visual guide to electrophysiology. Written for allied health personnel, including nurses, technologists, industry personnel, and new EP fellows, this book presents the most important aspects of the EP study using pertinent images accompanied by detailed discussions of the principles involved. Topics covered include hardware connections ("connectology"), catheter placement, intracardiac signals, normal electrogram sequences associated with sinus rhythm, andâ€”given an initial diagnosis of paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardiaâ€”the methodology we employ to uncover the mechanism of the tachycardia.</p>
<p>Because we have chosen to focus on the beginner, many complexities are either omitted or discussed in generalities only. The commentaries that follow most of the discussions throughout the book are intended to pique the reader's interest in advanced EP principles. Readers interested in gaining a more thorough understanding of these topics are referred to more comprehensive textbooks or to the current literature.</p>
<p>The computer/recording system used to generate the graphics in this guide may differ from the system with which you are familiar; however, the basic principles of signal processing (digitization, amplification, and filtering) remain the same. The channels displayed and their positions on the monitor are standard in our lab and may prove initially challenging if your lab organizes the display screen in a different manner. Regardless, try to focus on the information within these channels rather than on the cosmetics of how they are displayed. It is useful to interpret EP study findings no matter the format or from which center the recordings come, especially when preparing for EP certification examinations.</p>
<p>Teamwork in the EP lab is critical. In our lab, it is standard practice to encourage everyone to be involved and engaged in the diagnostic study and to be continually aware of any ECG changes, electrogram changes, or changes in catheter position. Checks and balances of this nature and a methodical, consistent approach are crucial components in the teamwork that enables any lab to run smoothly.</p>
<p>xiii</p>
<p>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="acknowledgments">Acknowledgments<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acknowledgments" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This textbook was initially inspired by my former cardiovascular technology student, Nicole Campbellâ€”now my colleagueâ€”who correctly observed during her EP rotation that there was no textbook geared to her needs. It was also inspired by a constant stream of new cardiac arrhythmia fellows who similarly wanted a collection of tracings illustrating the basic concepts of electrophysiology. Thus was created a workbook and improved curriculum for my medical classroom. The workbook was expanded over the years and used more informally during the training of EP students across Canada. The physicians on our arrhythmia service and my coauthors are outstanding teachers and operate in an atmosphere of openness, mutual respect, and trust with their teammates. This collaborative environment allowed me to learn â€œon the jobâ€ as well as during invigorating arrhythmia rounds.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="abbreviations-and-glossary">Abbreviations and Glossary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abbreviations-and-glossary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="accessory-pathway">Accessory pathway<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#accessory-pathway" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An additional electrical connection (other than the AV node) between the atria and ventricles</p>
<p>Accessory pathways may conduct:</p>
<p>Antegrade only - generates a delta wave
Retrograde only - no delta wave
Bi-directional - generates a delta wave</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anisotropy">Anisotropy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anisotropy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The concept that conduction velocity is determined by the angle of initial depolarization</p>
<p>xviii</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="antidromic">Antidromic<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antidromic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Denoting a wave of depolarization that is traveling retrogradely through the AV node. This term is usually used when describing the direction of the wavefront of an atrioventricular reentry circuit. In this scenario, the wave of depolarization travels down the accessory pathway and back up the AV node.</p>
<p>It can also denote a wave of depolarization that is traveling in the opposite direction to the predominate wave. For instance, a clockwise wave of depolarization introduced into a counterclockwise atrial flutter, therefore called an antidromic wavefront.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ap">AP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ap" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Accessory pathway</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ashman-s-phenomenon">Ashman's phenomenon<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ashman-s-phenomenon" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When a relatively long cycle (R-R) is followed by a relatively short R-R, the QRS associated with the short R-R often has right bundle branch block (RBBB) morphology. Often referred to as "long-shorting" the bundle branches. The right bundle branch has had insufficient time to adapt its ERP to a sudden change in heart rate. It therefore blocks.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-aeee9654.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="at">AT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#at" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Atrial tachycardia</p>

<p>A pacing maneuver where we pace slightly faster than the tachycardia cycle length, temporarily speed up the tachycardia circuit, then stop pacing to allow the circuit to return to its initial cycle length. The main purpose to determine how close to the tachycardia circuit the pacing catheter is.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="erp">ERP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#erp" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Effective refractory period â€“ the longest S1S2 that fails to conduct. Most of the time we are looking for the ERP of the AV node or an accessory pathway. With a very tight S1S2 (600, 200), you may reach the ERP of the myocardium itself (seen as no capture of the S2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fp">FP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fp" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>AV nodal fast pathway</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="frp">FRP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frp" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Functional refractory period</p>
<p>Examples:</p>
<ul>
<li>Referring to the AV node, it is the shortest output (H1-H2) achievable in response to any S1S2 input, which means that no decrement has occurred yet</li>
<li>Referring to the ventricle, it is the shortest V1-V2 achievable from any S1S2 input, which means no latency has occurred yet</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="gap">Gap<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gap" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An unexpected resumption of conduction after block has occurred</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="his">HIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#his" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The connecting cable between AV node and bundle branches</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="his-purkinje-system">His-Purkinje system<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#his-purkinje-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The specialized conduction system within the ventricles</p>

<p>High right atrium/high right atrial</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="h-v-interval">H-V interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#h-v-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Transit time from the His bundle to the ventricle</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ipsilateral">Ipsilateral<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ipsilateral" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>On the same side â€“ it often is used as â€œipsilateral bundle branch block.â€ It refers to a RBBB pattern during a right-sided tachycardia or a LBBB during a left-sided tachycardia and the effect of that bundle branch block on the tachycardia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="isoproterenol">Isoproterenol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#isoproterenol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Isuprel â€“ a sympathomimetic. Used to increase heart rate and used in the EP lab to alter autonomic tone in the AV node.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="jump">Jump<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#jump" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A sudden lengthening of the V-A time or A-V time of 50 msec or more with a tightening of the S2 coupling interval of 10 msec. A sudden, abrupt prolongation of the A-H interval.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="junctional">Junctional<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#junctional" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<p>No capture</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="orthodromic">Orthodromic<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#orthodromic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Denoting a wave of depolarization that is traveling antegradely through the AV node. Also a wave of depolarization traveling in the same direction as the predominant wavefront.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="overdrive-suppression">Overdrive suppression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#overdrive-suppression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When another focus within the heart spontaneously depolarizes faster than the SA node, the SA node is effectively put to sleep. Since the SA node drives a heart rate of 70 beats per minute and the junction drives the heart rate at 40 beats per minute, the SA node "overdrive suppresses" the junction. Similarly, a junctional escape rhythm of 40 beats per minute will overdrive suppress a ventricular escape rhythm of 20 beats per minute.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pac">PAC<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pac" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Premature atrial contraction</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="p-a-interval">P-A interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#p-a-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Transatrial conduction time</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="para-hisian-pacing">Para-Hisian pacing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#para-hisian-pacing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Refers to pacing beside or close to the bundle of His (para = beside or next to). A pacing maneuver used to determine the presence or absence of an antero-septal accessory pathway.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="paroxysmal">Paroxysmal<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paroxysmal" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Usually used as a descriptor of atrial fibrillation that lasts only a few hours and may be used with any tachycardia lasting only a few hours</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="persistent">Persistent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#persistent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Atrial fibrillation lasting days or longer</p>

<p>XXV</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="spontaneous-normalization">Spontaneous normalization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spontaneous-normalization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The ability of either bundle branch to adapt its conduction properties and thus transition from a LBBB or RBBB pattern to a normal QRS morphology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="svt">SVT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#svt" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tachycardia">Tachycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tachycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Rapid heart rate, usually more than 100 beats per minute</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tcl">TCL<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tcl" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Tachycardia cycle length</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transseptal-conduction">Transseptal conduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transseptal-conduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Refers to a wave of depolarization conducting across the interventricular septum. It can be from RV to LV or LV to RV. Such myocardial conduction is much slower than conduction through the specialized bundle branch system.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="t-wave">T wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#t-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ventricular repolarization on the ECG</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unipolar">Unipolar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unipolar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Refers to the use of a single pole (by convention, the positive electrode) in contact with the area of interest and a distant second electrode (by convention, the negative electrode). In essence, it is a VERY widely spaced bipole.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="v-a">V-A<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#v-a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>XXVI</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="wpw">WPW<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wpw" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Wolff-Parkinson-White (syndrome)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-db9edeb4.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="before-we-begin-an-svt-study">Before We Begin an SVT Study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#before-we-begin-an-svt-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are many reasons for patients to undergo an electrophysiology (EP) procedure. However, not every patient needs a full diagnostic study for supraventricular tachycardia (SVT), which involves incremental ventricular pacing followed by ventricular extra-stimulus pacing, atrial extra-stimulus pacing, and finally, atrial incremental pacing. Rather, the choice of catheters and equipment will change depending on the intent of the procedure. When the diagnosis has been clearly established, a more focused approach is employed. For example, if typical atrial flutter is the working diagnosis, our study will be abbreviated and focused as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Confirm, using entrainment, that it is indeed a typical, cavo-tricuspid isthmus (CTI)-dependent flutter.</li>
<li>Proceed with a CTI ablation.</li>
</ol>
<p>By contrast, if atrial fibrillation (AF) is the confirmed diagnosis, the diagnostic portion of the study will be very minimal to absent, and the ablation will be started after the appropriate setup.</p>
<p>The catheter setup and diagnostic techniques employed are unique and specific to the situation, but the SVT study forms the model for all studies and will be presented in more detail as such in the text to follow.</p>
<p>1</p>
<p>2</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="before-we-begin-an-svt-study-continued">Before We Begin an SVT Study continued<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#before-we-begin-an-svt-study-continued" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In this example, the clinical diagnosis was unclear so a full diagnostic study was performed. AV nodal reentrant tachycardia (AVNRT) was the final diagnosis. Remember, just because the patient demonstrates AVNRT does not eliminate the possibility that a substrate for a different tachycardia (eg, accessory pathway [AP]) exists. Our full diagnostic study ruled this out.</p>
<p>Commentary: Entrainment (mentioned on page 1) is covered later in this guide.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="a3184531">UNIVERSITÃ„T<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a3184531" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a3184531">UNIVERSITÃ„T<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a3184531" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a3184531">UNIVERSITÃ„T<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a3184531" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="unit-1-the-basics">Unit 1: The Basics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-1-the-basics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In this unit we look at catheter placement, the computer system, signal processing, signal sequence in sinus rhythm, basic conduction intervals, and two basic but critical tissue characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Conduction velocity</li>
<li>Refractoriness</li>
</ul>
<p>Understanding these concepts is critical to understanding the mechanisms of most tachycardias.</p>
<p>Starting on page 18, you will find our methodology and the sequence of cardiac stimulation we use in a routine diagnostic study for SVT. Examining the normal sequence of signal conduction and measuring various basic intervals establishes a baseline for the patient. These measurements will vary depending on the clinical problem.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-1-outline">UNIT 1 OUTLINE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-1-outline" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>UNIT 1 THE BASICS</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-catheter-placement">1. Catheter Placement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-catheter-placement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Multi-electrode catheters are exclusively used in clinical EP. Electrical signals are detected and recorded from the individual electrodes and from adjacent electrodes (bipole). By convention, the most distal electrode (at the tip of the catheter) is numbered 1. Subsequent electrodes are numbered in a sequential fashion, as shown in the coronary sinus (CS) decapolar catheter here (labeled in green).</p>
<p>The high right atrial (HRA) catheter is labeled in red. It will record an atrial (A) signal on the red HRA channel on our tracings throughout this book. The HIS catheter is labeled in yellow. It is positioned in the region of the atrioventricular (AV) node guided by both the signals and fluoroscopic images. Since the AV node is in close proximity to both the atria and the ventricles, it will have both an A signal as well as a ventricular (V) signal. In addition, the HIS catheter will have a third electrogram (EGM) representing the bundle of His, which is the electrical conduit from the AV node to the specialized conduction system within the ventricles. This EGM is often referred to simply as an "H." So the properly positioned HIS catheter will display three EGMsâ€”atrial (A), HIS (H), and ventricular (V)â€”on our yellow HIS channel. The right ventricular apex (RVA) catheter is labeled in magenta. It will display a V signal on our magenta RVA channel.</p>
<p>The CS catheter is labeled in green. The CS is the vein situated between the left atrium and the left ventricle. Therefore, this catheter, for the most part, identifies signals from the left atrium and left ventricle when properly positioned within the CS. It is our custom that CS 9-10 should be positioned at the left edge of the spine in the left anterior oblique (LAO) projection. This "neutral" position places these poles at the junction of the right atrium and the CS opening. CS 1-2 will display signals from the lateral aspect of the left atrium. Most CS signals will have two components, A and V, on our green CS channel. Currently, we use a 10-pole (decapolar) CS catheter.</p>
<p>Commentary: Atrial activation sequence proceeds from proximal to distal in the normal heart assuming a normally positioned (neutral or central) CS catheter. If the CS catheter is not in the neutral position, atrial activation during ventricular pacing may appear eccentric and thus be very misleading (ie, mimicking APs). If the CS catheter is advanced too far into the CS, the signals may appear as a chevron (ie, CS 1-2 activation is as early as CS 9-10 activation). This early activation is due to detection of Bachmann's bundle.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-05402272.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-3e713112.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg"></p>
<p>UNIT 1 THE BASICS</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-the-computer-system">2. The Computer System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-the-computer-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All intracardiac catheters are plugged into patient interface blocks via connecting cables. The electrical signals detected from the catheters are sent to these blocks. From here, the signals are sent to the amplifier and then on to the recording system and monitors.</p>
<p>How your computer/recording system displays these signals is quite customizable. The colors of individual EGMs, signal location on the screen, order of the catheters displayed on the screen, gains, and filters are entirely modifiable.</p>
<p>In this image, we have our CS catheter plugged into the interface block on the far right. To see these signals, we instruct the computer to display Block A &gt;&gt; pins 1 to 10 &gt;&gt; and color them green. We then position these bipole signals (CS 1-2, CS 3-4, CS 5-6, CS 7-8, CS 9-10) at the bottom of our screen.</p>
<p>When troubleshooting, keep in mind that problems often stem from a "connectology" issue. Be sure catheters are connected securely to the connection cables, cables to the interface boxes, and boxes to the amplifier. Also check that channel gains are appropriate, filters have not been changed, and someone didn't turn off the signal display. It is not uncommon to accidentally plug the catheters into the wrong pins on these blue interface boxes and thus display no signals at all.</p>
<p>Commentary: Filtering of signals is explained in greater detail on page 10.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-67c0d485.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg"></p>
<p>Connecting Cables</p>
<p>Connecting Cables to Improve the Quality of Life</p>
<p>Interfaced Clothes</p>
<p>Connecting Cables to Improve the Quality of Life</p>
<p>Connections to Amp, Recording system and Monitors</p>
<p>UNIT 1 THE BASICS</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-signal-processing">3. Signal Processing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-signal-processing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>How we see the signals on the monitor is a result of various processing procedures. You need to understand the concepts of gain, clipping, high-pass filters, and low-pass filters. This image shows the settings for electrocardiogram (ECG) filtering (channels 1â€“12) and three intracardiac channel settings (channels 33â€“48).</p>
<p>Gain simply increases the amplitude, or size, of the signal. It only amplifies whatever is on that channel, including (unfortunately) noise. Clipping is simply constraining the size of a signal to a geographic location on the monitor such that two adjacent signals don't overlap each other.</p>
<p>High-pass and low-pass filters are often confused. The high-pass filter allows any signals higher than the preset frequency (Hz) to be passed through to the monitor. Therefore, it filters out low-frequency signals, such as a wandering baseline. In this example, any intracardiac signal with a frequency higher than 30 Hz will be allowed to pass through to the monitor and any signal lower than 30 Hz will be blocked. The low-pass filter allows any signals less than the preset frequency to be passed through to the monitor. It, therefore, filters out high-frequency signals. In this example, any intracardiac signal with a frequency lower than 500 Hz will be allowed to pass through to the monitor and any signal higher than 500 Hz will be blocked.</p>
<p>The total effect is to allow signals between 30 and 500 Hz to be displayed. Signals outside this range are blocked from view.</p>
<p>The notch pass is a special and a relatively specific 60-Hz noise filter.</p>
<p>Commentary: Changing the high-pass filter on the ECG can markedly change its morphology. Try it and see the result. Highly filtered signals look "cleaner" but remove information, possibly important information. There is always a compromise between obtaining a clean signal and maintaining the information required. The filter settings displayed here are nominal and consistent across most recording systems.</p>

<p>UNIT 1 THE BASICS</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-signal-sequence-in-sinus-rhythm">4. Signal Sequence in Sinus Rhythm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-signal-sequence-in-sinus-rhythm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>1st: The HRA A electrogram (AEGM) is the earliest since this catheter is closest to the sino-atrial (SA) node. Note that it corresponds to the onset of the surface P-wave.</p>
<p>2nd: HIS A is the next AEGM to appear in sequence. The wave of atrial depolarization has successfully arrived at the AV node.</p>
<p>3rd: CS A signals appear next as the wave spreads from the proximal CS distally into the left atrium.</p>
<p>4th: The H deflection on the HIS catheter is next. This indicates that the wave has propagated over the atrioventricular (AV) node and arrived at the bundle of His.</p>
<p>5th: The RVA V electrogram (VEGM) is generally next as it is near to the right bundle branch exit, which is usually the first part of the ventricles to be activated. Note that it is before the HIS V or CS V signals since the wave of depolarization travels down the bundle branches, past the apex, and arrives at the base of the ventricles last.</p>
<p>6th: The HIS channel V and the CS V's are the last signals to occur. Remember that the HIS and CS catheters are positioned at the base of the ventricles.</p>
<p>Commentary: Take careful note that the RVA V signal is ahead of the HIS V and CS V signals in sinus rhythm since the right ventricle is activated early in normal depolarization without bundle branch block. On the other hand, the HIS or CS V signal may precede the right ventricle with right bundle branch block (RBBB), where the left ventricle will be activated before the right. This relationship could change if an AP is present and may give a clue as to the insertion site of the AP into the ventricles.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260120-75210e53.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg"></p>
<p>UNIT 1 THE BASICS</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-basic-conduction-intervals">5. Basic Conduction Intervals<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-basic-conduction-intervals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Routine baseline measurements should be made and recorded for every patient undergoing an EP study. The names and normal ranges are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>P-A interval: Measure from the onset of the P-wave on the surface ECG to the rapid deflection of the A-wave on the HIS channel. (See the red calipers.) The P-A interval is usually about 35 to 45 msec. This is the transright atrial conduction time, that is, the approximate time it takes for the electrical signal to travel from the SA node to the AV node.</li>
<li>A-H interval: Measure on the HIS channel as the A signal to the onset of the H deflection. (See the white calipers.) The A-H interval is usually about 70 to 80 msec. This is the transnodal conduction time, or the time it takes for the electrical signal to travel through the AV node.</li>
<li>H-V interval: Measure from the onset of the HIS deflection to the earliest onset of ventricular activation on any channel available, either intracardiac or ECG. (See the green calipers.) It is usually the onset of the QRS complex. The H-V interval is about 35 to 45 msec. This is the His to ventricular activation time, that is, the time it takes for the electrical signal to travel from the His bundle to the ventricles.</li>
</ul>
<p>Therefore, on a surface ECG, these three measurements make up the PR interval:</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable rowspacing="0.16em" columnalign="left" columnspacing="1em"><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mtext>PR&nbsp;interval</mtext><mo>=</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mo>âˆ’</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mo>+</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mo>âˆ’</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mo>+</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mo>âˆ’</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>40</mn><mo>+</mo><mn>80</mn><mo>+</mo><mn>40</mn></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>160</mn><mtext>&nbsp;msec</mtext></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\begin{array}{l}
\text{PR interval} = \mathrm{P} - \mathrm{A} + \mathrm{A} - \mathrm{H} + \mathrm{H} - \mathrm{V} \\
= 40 + 80 + 40 \\
= 160 \text{ msec}
\end{array}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 3.6em; vertical-align: -1.55em;"></span><span
